   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Lebanon</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country in Western Asia
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the country. For other uses of "Lebanon", see <a href="/wiki/Lebanon_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Lebanon (disambiguation)">Lebanon (disambiguation)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liban_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Liban (disambiguation)">Liban (disambiguation)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Libnan_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Libnan (disambiguation)">Libnan (disambiguation)</a>.
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       "Lebanese Republic" redirects here. It is not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Greater_Lebanon" title="Greater Lebanon">the predecessor state</a> between 1926 and 1946 of modern Lebanon.
      </div>

      <p> <span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: 
         <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r994658806"><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Lebanon&amp;params=33_50_N_35_50_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">33°50′N</span> <span class="longitude">35°50′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">33.833°N 35.833°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">33.833; 35.833</span></span></span></a></span></span></span><b>Lebanon</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-US-Lebanon.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/En-US-Lebanon.ogg" class="internal" title="En-US-Lebanon.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">لُبْنَان</span>‎ <i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">lubnān</i>, <small>Lebanese Arabic pronunciation:&nbsp;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Lebanese_Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Lebanese Arabic">[lɪbˈneːn]</a></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[12]</a></sup> officially known as the <b>Lebanese Republic</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[a]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle Eastern</a> country in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lebanon%E2%80%93Syria_border" title="Lebanon–Syria border">the north and east</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> to <a href="/wiki/Border_between_Israel_and_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Border between Israel and Syria">the south</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> lies just west of it across the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. Lebanon's location at the crossroads of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabian</a> <a href="/wiki/Hinterland" title="Hinterland">hinterland</a> has contributed to its <a href="/wiki/History_of_Lebanon" title="History of Lebanon">rich history</a> and shaped <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Lebanon" title="Culture of Lebanon">a cultural identity</a> of <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Lebanon#Religious_groups" title="Demographics of Lebanon">religious</a> diversity.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[13]</a></sup> It covers an area of just 10,452&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup> (4,036&nbsp;mi<sup>2</sup>), with a population of some 6 million, and is one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">smallest countries</a> in Asia. <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> form the vast majority of the country's population, while <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>, and spoken <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Arabic" title="Lebanese Arabic">Lebanese Arabic</a> is used alongside <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> in daily life. </p>
      <p>The earliest <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_record" title="Archaeological record">evidence</a> of civilization in Lebanon dates back more than seven thousand years, predating <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-byblos_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byblos-19">[14]</a></sup> Lebanon was home to the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a>, a maritime culture that flourished for almost three thousand years (<a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">c.</a> 3200–539 BC). In 64 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> conquered the region, and eventually became one of its leading centers of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a> range saw the emergence of a monastic tradition known as the <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Church" title="Maronite Church">Maronite Church</a>. As the <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_the_Levant" title="Muslim conquest of the Levant">conquered</a> the region, the <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronites</a> held onto their religion and <a href="/wiki/Religious_identity" title="Religious identity">identity</a>. However, a new religious group, the <a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a>, established themselves in Mount Lebanon as well, generating a religious divide that has lasted for centuries. During the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, the Maronites re-established contact with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic Church</a> and asserted their communion with <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Rome</a>. These ties have influenced the region into the modern era.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Lebanon was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a> in the 16th century and remained under their rule for the next 400 years. Following the empire's collapse after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the five provinces constituting modern Lebanon came under the <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="French Mandate of Lebanon">French Mandate</a>. The <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> expanded the borders of the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Governorate" title="Mount Lebanon Governorate">Mount Lebanon Governorate</a>, which was predominately Maronite and Druze, to include more Muslims.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Contraction: was Lebanon or mt Lebanon conquered (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Upon independence in 1943, Lebanon established a unique <a href="/wiki/Confessionalism_(politics)" title="Confessionalism (politics)">confessionalist</a> form of government, with the major religious sects <a href="/wiki/Consociationalism" title="Consociationalism">apportioned specific political powers</a>. Lebanon initially enjoyed political and economic stability, which was shattered by the bloody <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War" title="Lebanese Civil War">Lebanese Civil War</a> (1975–1990) between various political and sectarian factions. The war partially led to military occupations by <a href="/wiki/Syrian_occupation_of_Lebanon" title="Syrian occupation of Lebanon">Syria</a> (1975 to 2005) and <a href="/wiki/Israeli_occupation_of_Southern_Lebanon" title="Israeli occupation of Southern Lebanon">Israel</a> (1985 to 2000). </p>
      <p>Despite Lebanon's small size,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[15]</a></sup> Lebanese <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Lebanon" title="Culture of Lebanon">culture</a> is renowned both in the Arab world and globally, powered by its large and influential <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people" title="Lebanese people">diaspora.</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Prior to the civil war, the country enjoyed a diversified economy that included tourism, agriculture, commerce, and banking.<sup id="cite_ref-dos-2010-03-22_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dos-2010-03-22-21">[16]</a></sup> Its financial power and stability through the 1950s and 1960s earned Lebanon the name of "Switzerland of the East",<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[17]</a></sup> while its capital, <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, attracted so many tourists that it was known as "the <a href="/wiki/Paris_of_the_East_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris of the East (disambiguation)">Paris of the Middle East</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-tourism_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tourism-23">[18]</a></sup> Since the end of the war, there have been extensive efforts to revive the economy and rebuild national infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-Canada_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canada-24">[19]</a></sup> While still recovering from the political and economic effects of the conflict, Lebanon remains a cosmopolitan and developing country, with one of the highest levels of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index#Arab_League" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Middle_East#Statistics" title="Economy of the Middle East">GDP per capita</a> in the Arab world outside of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">oil-rich economies of the Persian Gulf</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lebanon was a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in 1945 and is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> (1945), the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> (1961), <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_the_Islamic_Cooperation" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation of the Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of the Islamic Cooperation</a> (1969), and the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_internationale_de_la_francophonie" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation internationale de la francophonie">Organisation internationale de la francophonie</a> (1973). </p>

         <ul> 
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Ancient_Lebanon"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Ancient Lebanon</span></a></li>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <p>The name of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a> originates from the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician</a> <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">root</a> <i><i lang="sem" title="Semitic languages collective text">lbn</i></i> (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%F0%90%A4%8B%F0%90%A4%81%F0%90%A4%8D" class="extiw" title="wikt:𐤋𐤁𐤍">𐤋𐤁𐤍</a>) meaning "white", apparently from its snow-capped peaks.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Occurrences of the name have been found in different <a href="/wiki/Middle_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Bronze Age">Middle Bronze Age</a> texts from the library of <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[22]</a></sup> and three of the twelve tablets of the <a href="/wiki/Epic_of_Gilgamesh" title="Epic of Gilgamesh">Epic of Gilgamesh</a>. The name is recorded in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Ancient Egyptian</a> as <i>Rmnn</i> (𓂋𓏠𓈖𓈖𓈉), where <i>R</i> stood for <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite</a> <i>L</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[23]</a></sup> The name occurs nearly 70 times in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, as <span lang="he" dir="rtl" title="Hebrew-language text">לְבָנוֹן</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Lebanon</i> as the name of an administrative unit (as opposed to the mountain range) that was introduced with the <a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Ottoman reforms</a> of 1861, as the <i><a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Mutasarrifate" title="Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate">Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">متصرفية جبل لبنان</span>‎; <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <i lang="tr">Cebel-i Lübnan Mutasarrıflığı</i>), continued in the name of the <i><a href="/wiki/Greater_Lebanon" title="Greater Lebanon">State of Greater Lebanon</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">دولة لبنان الكبير</span>‎ <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">Dawlat Lubnān al-Kabīr</i></i>; <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">État du Grand Liban</i>) in 1920, and eventually in the name of the sovereign <i>Republic of Lebanon</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">الجمهورية اللبنانية</span>‎ <i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization"><i>al-Jumhūrīyah al-Lubnānīyah</i></i>) upon its independence in 1943. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Lebanon" title="History of Lebanon">History of Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a>
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">
           This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Lebanon%22">"Lebanon"</a>&nbsp;–&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Lebanon%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Lebanon%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Lebanon%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Lebanon%22">scholar</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Lebanon%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>The borders of contemporary Lebanon are a product of the Treaty of Sèvres of 1920. Its territory was the core of the Bronze Age Phoenician (<a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>) city-states. As part of the Levant, it was part of numerous succeeding empires throughout ancient history, including the Egyptian, Assyrian, Babylonian, Achaemenid Persian, Hellenistic, Roman and Sasanid Persian empires. </p>
      <p>After the 7th-century Muslim conquest of the Levant, it was part of the Rashidun, Umayyad, Abbasid Seljuk and Fatimid empires. The crusader state of the County of Tripoli, founded by Raymond IV of Toulouse in 1102, encompassed most of present-day Lebanon, falling to the Mamluk Sultanate in 1289 and finally to the Ottoman Empire in 1516.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup> With the dissolution of the Ottoman Empire, Greater Lebanon fell under French mandate in 1920,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[26]</a></sup> and gained independence under president Bechara El Khoury in 1943. Lebanon's history since independence has been marked by alternating periods of political stability and prosperity based on Beirut's position as a regional center for finance and trade, interspersed with political turmoil and armed conflict (1948 Arab–Israeli War, Lebanese Civil War 1975–1990, 2005 Cedar Revolution, 2006 Lebanon War, 2007 Lebanon conflict, 2006–08 Lebanese protests, 2008 conflict in Lebanon, 2011 Syrian Civil War spillover, and 2019–20 Lebanese protests).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[27]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Lebanon">Ancient Lebanon</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Lebanon" title="History of ancient Lebanon">History of ancient Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Temple_of_Bacchus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Temple_of_Bacchus.jpg/220px-Temple_of_Bacchus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Temple_of_Bacchus.jpg/330px-Temple_of_Bacchus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Temple_of_Bacchus.jpg/440px-Temple_of_Bacchus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3840" data-file-height="2160"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Temple_of_Bacchus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Bacchus" title="Temple of Bacchus">Temple of Bacchus</a> is located in <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Baalbek</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:PhoenicianTrade_EN.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/PhoenicianTrade_EN.svg/220px-PhoenicianTrade_EN.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/PhoenicianTrade_EN.svg/330px-PhoenicianTrade_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/PhoenicianTrade_EN.svg/440px-PhoenicianTrade_EN.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:PhoenicianTrade_EN.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map of Phoenicia and trade routes
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Evidence dating back to an early settlement in Lebanon was found in <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a>, considered one of the <a href="/wiki/Oldest_continuously_inhabited_cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Oldest continuously inhabited cities">oldest continuously inhabited cities</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-byblos_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byblos-19">[14]</a></sup> The evidence dates back to earlier than 5000 BC. Archaeologists discovered remnants of prehistoric huts with crushed limestone floors, primitive weapons, and <a href="/wiki/Jar_burial" title="Jar burial">burial jars</a> left by the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper Age">Chalcolithic</a> fishing communities who lived on the shore of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> Sea over 7,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lebanon was part of northern <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>, and consequently became the homeland of Canaanite descendants, the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a>, a seafaring people who spread across the Mediterranean in the first millennium BC.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup> The most prominent Phoenician cities were <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, while their most famous colonies were <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> and <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">Cádiz</a> in present-day Spain. The <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Phoenicians</a> are credited with the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">oldest verified alphabet</a>, which subsequently inspired the Greek alphabet and the Latin one thereafter.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Proto-Canaanite is cited as the oldest alphabet (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The cities of Phoenicia were incorporated into the Persian <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> in 539 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-35">[30]</a></sup> The Phoenician city-states were later incorporated into the empire of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tyre_(332_BC)" title="Siege of Tyre (332 BC)">Siege of Tyre</a> in 332 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-35">[30]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Lebanon">Medieval Lebanon</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Byblos_Libanon_2003.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Byblos_Libanon_2003.JPG/220px-Byblos_Libanon_2003.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Byblos_Libanon_2003.JPG/330px-Byblos_Libanon_2003.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Byblos_Libanon_2003.JPG/440px-Byblos_Libanon_2003.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1840" data-file-height="1232"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Byblos_Libanon_2003.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a> is believed to have been first occupied between 8800 and 7000<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>BC<sup id="cite_ref-PeltenburgWasse2004a_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PeltenburgWasse2004a-36">[31]</a></sup> and continuously inhabited since 5000<span class="nowrap">&nbsp;</span>BC,<sup id="cite_ref-byblos1_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byblos1-37">[32]</a></sup> making it one of <a href="/wiki/List_of_oldest_continuously_inhabited_cities" title="List of oldest continuously inhabited cities">the oldest continuously inhabited cities in the world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-38">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Siege_of_Tripoli_Painting_(1289).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Siege_of_Tripoli_Painting_%281289%29.jpg/170px-Siege_of_Tripoli_Painting_%281289%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Siege_of_Tripoli_Painting_%281289%29.jpg/255px-Siege_of_Tripoli_Painting_%281289%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Siege_of_Tripoli_Painting_%281289%29.jpg/340px-Siege_of_Tripoli_Painting_%281289%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1720" data-file-height="2725"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Siege_of_Tripoli_Painting_(1289).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Tripoli_(1289)" title="Fall of Tripoli (1289)">Fall of Tripoli</a> to the Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluks</a> and destruction of the Crusader state, the County of Tripoli, 1289
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The region that is now Lebanon, as with the rest of Syria and much of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, became a major center of Christianity in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> during the early spread of the faith. During the late 4th and early 5th century, a hermit named <a href="/wiki/Maron" title="Maron">Maron</a> established a monastic tradition focused on the importance of <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">asceticism</a>, near the Mediterranean mountain range known as <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a>. The monks who followed Maron spread his teachings among Lebanese in the region. These Christians came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Maronite" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite">Maronites</a> and moved into the mountains to avoid religious persecution by Roman authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup> During the frequent <a href="/wiki/Roman-Persian_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman-Persian Wars">Roman-Persian Wars</a> that lasted for many centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Persians</a> occupied what is now Lebanon from 619 till 629.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the 7th century the Muslim Arabs <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquest of Syria">conquered Syria</a> establishing a new regime to replace the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantines</a>. Though Islam and the Arabic language were officially dominant under this new regime, the general populace nonetheless only gradually converted from Christianity and the Syriac language. The Maronite community, in particular, managed to maintain a large degree of autonomy despite the succession of rulers over Lebanon and Syria. </p>
      <p>The relative (but not complete) isolation of the Lebanese mountains meant the mountains served as a refuge in the times of religious and political crises in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. As such, the mountains displayed religious diversity and existence of several well established sects and religions, notably, <a href="/wiki/Maronites" title="Maronites">Maronites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shiite Muslims</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">Ismailis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alawites" title="Alawites">Alawites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Jacobites</a>. </p>
      <p>During the 11th century the <a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a> religion emerged from a branch of <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a>. The new religion gained followers in the southern portion of Mount Lebanon. The southern portion of Mount Lebanon was ruled by Druze feudal families to the early 14th century. The Maronite population increased gradually in Northern Mount Lebanon and the Druze have remained in Southern Mount Lebanon until the modern era. <a href="/wiki/Keserwan_District" title="Keserwan District">Keserwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jabal_Amel" title="Jabal Amel">Jabal Amel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Beqaa_Valley" title="Beqaa Valley">Beqaa Valley</a> was ruled by Shia feudal families under the Mamluks and the Ottoman Empire. Major cities on the coast, <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripoli,_Lebanon" title="Tripoli, Lebanon">Tripoli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>, and others, were directly administered by the Muslim Caliphs and the people became more fully absorbed by the Arab culture. </p>
      <p>Following the fall of Roman <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> to the Muslim Turks, the Byzantines put out a call to the Pope in Rome for assistance in the 11th century. The result was a series of wars known as the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> launched by the <a href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankish Empire">Franks</a> from Western Europe to reclaim the former Byzantine Christian territories in the Eastern Mediterranean, especially <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a></i>). The <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> succeeded in temporarily establishing the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/County_of_Tripoli" title="County of Tripoli">County of Tripoli</a> as Roman Catholic Christian states along the coast.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup> These crusader states made a lasting impact on the region, though their control was limited, and the region returned to full Muslim control after two centuries following the conquest by the Mamluks. </p>
      <p>One of the most lasting effects of the Crusades in this region was the contact between the Franks (i.e. the French) and the Maronites. Unlike most other Christian communities in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>, who swore allegiance to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> or other local patriarchs, the Maronites proclaimed allegiance to the Pope in Rome. As such the Franks saw them as Roman Catholic brethren. These initial contacts led to centuries of support for the Maronites from France and Italy, even after the fall of the Crusader states in the region. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Lebanon_and_French_Mandate">Ottoman Lebanon and French Mandate</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Mount_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Emirate of Mount Lebanon">Emirate of Mount Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidon_Eyalet" title="Sidon Eyalet">Sidon Eyalet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Mutasarrifate" title="Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate">Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:DeirAlQamar-FakhredinePalace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/DeirAlQamar-FakhredinePalace.jpg/220px-DeirAlQamar-FakhredinePalace.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/DeirAlQamar-FakhredinePalace.jpg/330px-DeirAlQamar-FakhredinePalace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/DeirAlQamar-FakhredinePalace.jpg/440px-DeirAlQamar-FakhredinePalace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">

        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg/170px-Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg/255px-Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg/340px-Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1820" data-file-height="2400"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>1862 map drawn by the French expedition of <a href="/wiki/Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul" title="Charles-Marie-Napoléon de Beaufort d'Hautpoul">Beaufort d'Hautpoul</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Hakim2013_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hakim2013-44">[39]</a></sup> later used as a template for the 1920 borders of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Lebanon" title="Greater Lebanon">Greater Lebanon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Firro2003_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firro2003-45">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rooke_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooke-46">[41]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During this period Lebanon was divided into several provinces: Northern and Southern Mount Lebanon, Tripoli, Baalbek and Beqaa Valley, and <a href="/wiki/Jabal_Amel" title="Jabal Amel">Jabal Amel</a>. </p>
      <p>In southern Mount Lebanon in 1590, <a href="/wiki/Fakhr-al-Din_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Fakhr-al-Din II">Fakhr-al-Din II</a> became the successor to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Emirate#Qurqumaz_(1544–1585)" title="Mount Lebanon Emirate">Korkmaz</a>. He soon established his authority as paramount prince of the Druze in the Shouf area of Mount Lebanon. Eventually, Fakhr-al-Din II was appointed <a href="/wiki/Sanjak-bey" title="Sanjak-bey">Sanjakbey</a> (Governor) of several Ottoman sub-provinces, with responsibility for tax-gathering. He extended his control over a substantial part of Mount Lebanon and its coastal area, even building a fort as far inland as <a href="/wiki/Palmyra_Castle" title="Palmyra Castle">Palmyra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup> This over-reaching eventually became too much for Ottoman Sultan <a href="/wiki/Murad_IV" title="Murad IV">Murad IV</a>, who sent a punitive expedition to capture him in 1633. He was taken to <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, kept in prison for two years and then executed along with one of his sons in April 1635.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup> Surviving members of Fakhr al-Din's family ruled a reduced area under closer Ottoman control until the end of the 17th century. </p>
      <p>On the death of the last Maan emir, various members of the Shihab clan ruled Mount Lebanon until 1830. Approximately 10,000 Christians <a href="/wiki/1860_Lebanon_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="1860 Lebanon conflict">were killed</a> by the Druzes during inter-communal violence in 1860.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Mount_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Emirate of Mount Lebanon">Emirate of Mount Lebanon</a>, which lasted about 400 years, was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Mutasarrifate" title="Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate">Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate</a>, as a result of a European-Ottoman treaty called the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A8glement_Organique_(Lebanon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Règlement Organique (Lebanon)">Règlement Organique</a>. The Baalbek and Beqaa Valley and Jabal Amel was ruled intermittently by various Shia feudal families, especially the Al Ali Alsagheer in Jabal Amel that remained in power until 1865 when Ottomans took direct ruling of the region. <a href="/wiki/Youssef_Bey_Karam" title="Youssef Bey Karam">Youssef Bey Karam</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup> a Lebanese nationalist played an influential role in Lebanon's independence during this era. </p>
      <p>Around 100,000 people in Beirut and Mount Lebanon <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_Mount_Lebanon" title="Great Famine of Mount Lebanon">died</a> of starvation during World War I.<sup id="cite_ref-Ilah_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ilah-51">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1920, following <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the area of the Mutasarrifate, plus some surrounding areas which were predominantly <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia Islam in Lebanon">Shia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni</a>, became a part of the state of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Lebanon" title="Greater Lebanon">Greater Lebanon</a> under the <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_of_Syria_and_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="French Mandate of Syria and Lebanon">French Mandate of Syria and Lebanon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ilah_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ilah-51">[46]</a></sup> In the first half of 1920, Lebanese territory was claimed as part of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Kingdom_of_Syria" title="Arab Kingdom of Syria">Arab Kingdom of Syria</a>, but shortly the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Syrian_War" title="Franco-Syrian War">Franco-Syrian War</a> resulted in Arab defeat and capitulation of the Hashemites. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg/220px-French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg/330px-French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg/440px-French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="922" data-file-height="762"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map of the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon" title="Mandate for Syria and the Lebanon">French Mandate</a> and the states created in 1920
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 1 September 1920, France reestablished <a href="/wiki/Greater_Lebanon" title="Greater Lebanon">Greater Lebanon</a> after the Moutasarrifiya rule removed several regions belonging to the Principality of Lebanon and gave them to Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup> Lebanon was a largely Christian country (mainly <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronite</a> territory with some <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox</a> enclaves) but it also included areas containing many Muslims and <a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[48]</a></sup> On 1 September 1926, France formed the Lebanese Republic. A constitution was adopted on 25 May 1926 establishing a democratic republic with a parliamentary system of government. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_from_France">Independence from France</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22,_1943,_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22%2C_1943%2C_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22%2C_1943%2C_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22%2C_1943%2C_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1041" data-file-height="1836"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22,_1943,_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Martyrs%27_Square,_Beirut" title="Martyrs' Square, Beirut">Martyrs' Square in Beirut</a> during celebrations marking the release by the French of Lebanon's government from <a href="/wiki/Rashayya_prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Rashayya prison">Rashayya prison</a> on 22 November 1943
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lebanon gained a measure of independence while France was occupied by Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> General <a href="/wiki/Henri_Dentz" title="Henri Dentz">Henri Dentz</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy</a> <a href="/wiki/High_Commissioner" class="mw-redirect" title="High Commissioner">High Commissioner</a> for Syria and Lebanon, played a major role in the independence of the nation. The Vichy authorities in 1941 allowed Germany to move aircraft and supplies through <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> where they were used against British forces. The United Kingdom, fearing that <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> would gain full control of Lebanon and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> by pressure on the weak Vichy government, sent its army into Syria and Lebanon.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the fighting ended in Lebanon, General <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> visited the area. Under political pressure from both inside and outside Lebanon, de Gaulle recognized the independence of Lebanon. On 26 November 1941, General <a href="/wiki/Georges_Catroux" title="Georges Catroux">Georges Catroux</a> announced that Lebanon would become independent under the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Free_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French">Free French</a> government. Elections were held in 1943 and on 8 November 1943 the new Lebanese government unilaterally abolished the mandate. The French reacted by imprisoning the new government. In the face of international pressure, the French released the government officials on 22 November 1943. The <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">allies</a> occupied the region until the end of World War II. </p>
      <p>Following the end of World War II in Europe the French mandate may be said to have been terminated without any formal action on the part of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> or its successor the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>. The mandate was ended by the declaration of the mandatory power, and of the new states themselves, of their independence, followed by a process of piecemeal unconditional recognition by other powers, culminating in formal admission to the United Nations. Article 78 of the UN Charter ended the status of tutelage for any member state: "The trusteeship system shall not apply to territories which have become Members of the United Nations, relationship among which shall be based on respect for the principle of sovereign equality."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup> So when the UN officially came into existence on 24 October 1945, after ratification of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Charter">United Nations Charter</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_members_of_the_United_Nations_Security_Council" title="Permanent members of the United Nations Security Council">five permanent members</a>, as both Syria and Lebanon were founding member states, the French mandate for both was legally terminated on that date and full independence attained.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup> The last French troops withdrew in December 1946. </p>
      <p>Lebanon's unwritten <a href="/wiki/National_Pact" title="National Pact">National Pact</a> of 1943 required that its president be Maronite Christian, its <a href="/wiki/Speaker_(politics)" title="Speaker (politics)">speaker of the parliament</a> to be a <a href="/wiki/Shi%27a_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi'a Islam in Lebanon">Shia Muslim</a>, its prime minister be <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni Muslim</a>, and the Deputy Speaker of Parliament and the Deputy Prime Minister be <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet,_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg/220px-Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg/330px-Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg/440px-Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2593" data-file-height="2577"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet,_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Beirut in 1950
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lebanon's history since independence has been marked by alternating periods of political stability and turmoil interspersed with prosperity built on <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>'s position as a regional center for finance and trade.<sup id="cite_ref-dos-2009-01_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dos-2009-01-59">[54]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In May 1948, Lebanon supported neighbouring Arab countries in a <a href="/wiki/1948_Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_War" title="1948 Arab–Israeli War">war against Israel</a>. While some irregular forces crossed the border and carried out minor skirmishes against Israel, it was without the support of the Lebanese government, and Lebanese troops did not officially invade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris2008524_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris2008524-60">[55]</a></sup> Lebanon agreed to support the forces with covering artillery fire, armored cars, volunteers and logistical support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris2008259_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris2008259-61">[56]</a></sup> On 5–6 June 1948, the Lebanese army – led by the then <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="Minister of Defense">Minister of National Defence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emir_Majid_Arslan_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Emir Majid Arslan II">Emir Majid Arslan</a> – captured <a href="/wiki/Al-Malkiyya" title="Al-Malkiyya">Al-Malkiyya</a>. This was Lebanon's only success in the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris2008260_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris2008260-62">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>100,000 <a href="/wiki/Palestinians_in_Lebanon" title="Palestinians in Lebanon">Palestinians</a> fled to Lebanon because of the war. Israel <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_right_of_return" title="Palestinian right of return">did not permit their return</a> after the cease-fire.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-63">[58]</a></sup> As of 2017 between 174,000 and 450,000 Palestinian refugees live in Lebanon with about half in refugee camps (although these are often decades old and resemble neighborhoods).<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera-64">[59]</a></sup> Palestinians often cannot obtain <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationality_law" title="Lebanese nationality law">Lebanese citizenship</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_identity_card" title="Lebanese identity card">Lebanese identity cards</a> and are legally barred from owning property or performing certain occupations (including law, medicine, and engineering).<sup id="cite_ref-Butters_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butters-65">[60]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a>, Palestinian refugees in Lebanon live in "appalling social and economic conditions." </p>
      <p>In 1958, during the last months of President <a href="/wiki/Camille_Chamoun" title="Camille Chamoun">Camille Chamoun</a>'s term, <a href="/wiki/1958_Lebanon_crisis" title="1958 Lebanon crisis">an insurrection</a> broke out, instigated by Lebanese Muslims who wanted to make Lebanon a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Republic" title="United Arab Republic">United Arab Republic</a>. Chamoun requested assistance, and 5,000 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marines" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Marines">United States Marines</a> were briefly dispatched to Beirut on 15 July. After the crisis, a new government was formed, led by the popular former general <a href="/wiki/Fuad_Chehab" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuad Chehab">Fuad Chehab</a>. </p>
      <p>With the 1970 <a href="/wiki/Black_September_in_Jordan" class="mw-redirect" title="Black September in Jordan">defeat</a> of the <a href="/wiki/PLO" class="mw-redirect" title="PLO">PLO</a> in Jordan, many Palestinian militants relocated to Lebanon, increasing their <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_insurgency_in_South_Lebanon" title="Palestinian insurgency in South Lebanon">armed campaign</a> against Israel. The relocation of Palestinian bases also led to increasing sectarian tensions between Palestinians versus the Maronites and other Lebanese factions. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war_and_occupation">Civil war and occupation</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War" title="Lebanese Civil War">Lebanese Civil War</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Green_Line,_Beirut_1982.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg/220px-Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg/330px-Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg/440px-Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1065"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Green_Line,_Beirut_1982.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Green_Line_(Lebanon)" title="Green Line (Lebanon)">Green Line</a> that separated west and east Beirut, 1982
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1975, following increasing sectarian tensions, largely boosted by <a href="/wiki/Fatahland" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatahland">Palestinian militant relocation</a> into South Lebanon, a full-scale civil war broke out in Lebanon. The <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War" title="Lebanese Civil War">Lebanese Civil War</a> pitted a coalition of Christian groups against the joint forces of the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Liberation_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian Liberation Organization">PLO</a>, left-wing Druze and Muslim militias. In June 1976, Lebanese President <a href="/wiki/Elias_Sarkis" class="mw-redirect" title="Elias Sarkis">Elias Sarkis</a> asked for the Syrian Army to intervene on the side of the Christians and help restore peace.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup> In October 1976 the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> agreed to establish a predominantly Syrian <a href="/wiki/Arab_Deterrent_Force" title="Arab Deterrent Force">Arab Deterrent Force</a>, which was charged with restoring calm.<sup id="cite_ref-fco-cpl_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fco-cpl-67">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>PLO attacks from Lebanon into Israel in 1977 and 1978 escalated tensions between the countries. On 11 March 1978, eleven Fatah fighters landed on a beach in northern Israel and proceeded to hijack two buses full of passengers on the Haifa – Tel-Aviv road, shooting at passing vehicles in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Coastal_Road_massacre" title="Coastal Road massacre">Coastal Road massacre</a>. They killed 37 and wounded 76 Israelis before being killed in a firefight with Israeli forces.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup> Israel invaded Lebanon four days later in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Litani" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Litani">Operation Litani</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Israeli Army">Israeli Army</a> occupied most of the area south of the <a href="/wiki/Litani_River" title="Litani River">Litani River</a>. The <a href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Security Council">UN Security Council</a> passed <a href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council_Resolution_425" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Security Council Resolution 425">Resolution 425</a> calling for immediate Israeli withdrawal and creating the <a href="/wiki/UN_Interim_Force_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Interim Force in Lebanon">UN Interim Force in Lebanon</a> (UNIFIL), charged with attempting to establish peace. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon,_Al_Yatun_7-16_(1981).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Lebanon%2C_Al_Yatun_7-16_%281981%29.jpg/220px-Lebanon%2C_Al_Yatun_7-16_%281981%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Lebanon%2C_Al_Yatun_7-16_%281981%29.jpg/330px-Lebanon%2C_Al_Yatun_7-16_%281981%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Lebanon%2C_Al_Yatun_7-16_%281981%29.jpg/440px-Lebanon%2C_Al_Yatun_7-16_%281981%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1722" data-file-height="1119"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon,_Al_Yatun_7-16_(1981).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/UNIFIL" class="mw-redirect" title="UNIFIL">UNIFIL</a> base, 1981
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:BlueLine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/BlueLine.jpg/220px-BlueLine.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/BlueLine.jpg/330px-BlueLine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/BlueLine.jpg/440px-BlueLine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1299" data-file-height="898"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:BlueLine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map showing the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Line_(Lebanon)" title="Blue Line (Lebanon)">Blue Line</a> demarcation line between Lebanon and Israel, established by the UN after the Israeli withdrawal from southern Lebanon in 1978
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Israeli forces withdrew later in 1978, but retained control of the southern region by managing a 12-mile (19&nbsp;km) wide security zone along the border. These positions were held by the <a href="/wiki/South_Lebanon_Army" title="South Lebanon Army">South Lebanon Army</a> (SLA), a Christian-Shi'a militia under the leadership of Major <a href="/wiki/Saad_Haddad" title="Saad Haddad">Saad Haddad</a> backed by Israel. The Israeli Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Likud" title="Likud">Likud</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Menachem_Begin" title="Menachem Begin">Menachem Begin</a>, compared the plight of the Christian minority in southern Lebanon (then about 5% of the population in SLA territory) to that of European Jews during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[64]</a></sup> The PLO routinely <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_insurgency_in_South_Lebanon" title="Palestinian insurgency in South Lebanon">attacked</a> Israel during the period of the cease-fire, with over 270 documented attacks.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> People in Galilee regularly had to leave their homes during these shellings. Documents captured in PLO headquarters after the invasion showed they had come from Lebanon.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup> Arafat refused to condemn these attacks on the grounds that the cease-fire was only relevant to Lebanon.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Civil_war_Lebanon_map_1983a.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Civil_war_Lebanon_map_1983a.gif/200px-Civil_war_Lebanon_map_1983a.gif" decoding="async" width="200" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Civil_war_Lebanon_map_1983a.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="243" data-file-height="261"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Civil_war_Lebanon_map_1983a.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map showing power balance in Lebanon, 1983: Green – controlled by <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, purple – controlled by Christian groups, yellow – controlled by Israel, blue – controlled by the UN
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In April 1980 the presence of UNIFIL soldiers in the buffer zone led to the <a href="/wiki/At_Tiri_incident" title="At Tiri incident">At Tiri incident</a>. On 17 July 1981, Israeli aircraft bombed multi-story apartment buildings in Beirut that contained offices of PLO associated groups. The Lebanese delegate to the United Nations Security Council claimed that 300 civilians had been killed and 800 wounded. The bombing led to worldwide condemnation, and a temporary embargo on the export of U.S. aircraft to Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup> In August 1981, defense minister <a href="/wiki/Ariel_Sharon" title="Ariel Sharon">Ariel Sharon</a> began to draw up plans to attack PLO military infrastructure in West Beirut, where PLO headquarters and command bunkers were located.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1982, the <a href="/wiki/PLO_in_Lebanon" title="PLO in Lebanon">PLO</a> attacks from Lebanon on Israel led to <a href="/wiki/1982_Lebanon_War" title="1982 Lebanon War">an Israeli invasion</a>, aiming to support Lebanese forces in driving out the PLO. A <a href="/wiki/Multinational_Force_in_Lebanon" title="Multinational Force in Lebanon">multinational force</a> of American, French and Italian contingents (joined in 1983 by a British contingent) were deployed in Beirut after <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Beirut" title="Siege of Beirut">the Israeli siege of the city</a>, to supervise the evacuation of the PLO. The civil war re-emerged in September 1982 after the assassination of Lebanese President <a href="/wiki/Bashir_Gemayel" class="mw-redirect" title="Bashir Gemayel">Bashir Gemayel</a>, an Israeli ally, and subsequent fighting. During this time a number of sectarian massacres occurred, such as in <a href="/wiki/Sabra_and_Shatila_massacre" title="Sabra and Shatila massacre">Sabra and Shatila</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="War of the camps">several refugee camps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup> The multinational force was withdrawn in the spring of 1984, following <a href="/wiki/1983_Beirut_barracks_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="1983 Beirut barracks bombing">a devastating bombing attack</a> during the previous year. </p>
      <p>In September 1988, the Parliament failed to elect a successor to President Gemayel as a result of differences between the Christians, Muslims, and Syrians. The Arab League Summit of May 1989 led to the formation of a Saudi–Moroccan–Algerian committee to solve the crisis. On 16 September 1989 the committee issued a peace plan which was accepted by all. A ceasefire was established, the ports and airports were re-opened and refugees began to return.<sup id="cite_ref-fco-cpl_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fco-cpl-67">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the same month, the Lebanese Parliament agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Taif_Agreement" title="Taif Agreement">Taif Agreement</a>, which included an outline timetable for Syrian withdrawal from Lebanon and a formula for the de-confessionalisation of the Lebanese political system.<sup id="cite_ref-fco-cpl_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fco-cpl-67">[62]</a></sup> The civil war ended at the end of 1990 after sixteen years; it had caused massive loss of human life and property, and devastated the country's economy. It is estimated that 150,000 people were killed and another 200,000 wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[71]</a></sup> Nearly a million civilians were displaced by the war, and some never returned.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup> Parts of Lebanon were left in ruins.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> The Taif Agreement has still not been implemented in full and Lebanon's political system continues to be divided along sectarian lines. </p>
      <p>Conflict between Israel and the Lebanese resistance (mainly <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amal_Movement" title="Amal Movement">Amal movement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Communist_Party" title="Lebanese Communist Party">Lebanese Communist Party</a> ) continued leading to a series of violent events, including the <a href="/wiki/Qana_massacre" title="Qana massacre">Qana massacre</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80">[75]</a></sup> and to big losses.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup> In 2000, the Israeli forces withdrew from Lebanon .<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup> It estimated that more than 17,000 civilians were killed and more than 30,000 were injured. Since then, the 25 May is considered by the Lebanese as the <a href="/wiki/Liberation_Day_(Lebanon)" title="Liberation Day (Lebanon)">Liberation Day</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-80">[75]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Syrian_occupation_of_Lebanon" title="Syrian occupation of Lebanon">Syrian occupation of Lebanon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israeli_occupation_of_Southern_Lebanon" title="Israeli occupation of Southern Lebanon">Israeli occupation of Southern Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg/170px-Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg/255px-Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8c/Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg 2x" data-file-width="305" data-file-height="410"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Demonstrators calling for the withdrawal of Syrian forces.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The internal political situation in Lebanon significantly changed in the early 2000s. After the Israeli withdrawal from southern Lebanon and the death of former president <a href="/wiki/Hafez_Al-Assad" class="mw-redirect" title="Hafez Al-Assad">Hafez Al-Assad</a> in 2000, the Syrian military presence faced criticism and resistance from the Lebanese population.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 14 February 2005, former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Rafik_Hariri" class="mw-redirect" title="Rafik Hariri">Rafik Hariri</a> was assassinated in a <a href="/wiki/Car_bomb" title="Car bomb">car bomb</a> explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[83]</a></sup> Leaders of the <a href="/wiki/March_14_Alliance" title="March 14 Alliance">March 14 Alliance</a> accused <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> of the attack,<sup id="cite_ref-Syria-assassin_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Syria-assassin-89">[84]</a></sup> while Syria and the <a href="/wiki/March_8_Alliance" title="March 8 Alliance">March 8 Alliance</a> claimed that Israel was behind the assassination. The Hariri assassination marked the beginning of a series of assassinations that resulted in the death of many prominent Lebanese figures.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[nb 5]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The assassination triggered the <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Revolution" title="Cedar Revolution">Cedar Revolution</a>, a series of demonstrations which demanded the withdrawal of Syrian troops from Lebanon and the establishment of an international commission to investigate the assassination. Under pressure from the West, Syria began withdrawing,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[85]</a></sup> and by 26 April 2005 all Syrian soldiers had returned to Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[86]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1595" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1595">UNSC Resolution 1595</a> called for an investigation into the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/UN_International_Independent_Investigation_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="UN International Independent Investigation Commission">UN International Independent Investigation Commission</a> published preliminary findings on 20 October 2005 in the <a href="/wiki/Mehlis_report" title="Mehlis report">Mehlis report</a>, which cited indications that the assassination was organized by Syrian and Lebanese intelligence services.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_S2005662_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_S2005662-96">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 12 July 2006, <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a> launched a series of rocket attacks and <a href="/wiki/2006_Hezbollah_cross-border_raid" title="2006 Hezbollah cross-border raid">raids</a> into Israeli territory, where they killed three Israeli soldiers and captured two others.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup> Israel responded with <a href="/wiki/Airstrike" title="Airstrike">airstrikes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> fire on targets in Lebanon, and a ground invasion of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Lebanon" title="Southern Lebanon">southern Lebanon</a>, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/2006_Lebanon_War" title="2006 Lebanon War">2006 Lebanon War</a>. The conflict was officially ended by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1701" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1701">UNSC Resolution 1701</a> on 14 August 2006, which ordered a ceasefire.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> Some 1,191 Lebanese<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> and 160 Israelis<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-101">[95]</a></sup> were <a href="/wiki/Casualties_of_the_2006_Lebanon_War" title="Casualties of the 2006 Lebanon War">killed</a> in the conflict. Beirut's southern suburb was heavily damaged by Israeli airstrikes.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cedar_Revolution_Demonstrators.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cedar_Revolution_Demonstrators.jpg/220px-Cedar_Revolution_Demonstrators.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cedar_Revolution_Demonstrators.jpg/330px-Cedar_Revolution_Demonstrators.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cedar_Revolution_Demonstrators.jpg/440px-Cedar_Revolution_Demonstrators.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cedar_Revolution_Demonstrators.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
