         </div>Demonstrations in Lebanon triggered by the assassination of the former Lebanese Prime Minister Rafik Hariri on February 14, 2005
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Instability_and_Syrian_War_spillover">Instability and Syrian War spillover</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War_spillover_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian Civil War spillover in Lebanon">Syrian Civil War spillover in Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Lebanese_protests" title="2011 Lebanese protests">2011 Lebanese protests</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2019%E2%80%9320_Lebanese_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2019–20 Lebanese protests">2019–20 Lebanese protests</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Nahr_al-Bared" title="Nahr al-Bared">Nahr al-Bared</a> refugee camp became the center of the <a href="/wiki/2007_Lebanon_conflict" title="2007 Lebanon conflict">2007 Lebanon conflict</a> between the Lebanese Army and <a href="/wiki/Fatah_al-Islam" title="Fatah al-Islam">Fatah al-Islam</a>. At least 169 soldiers, 287 insurgents and 47 civilians were killed in the battle. Funds for the reconstruction of the area have been slow to materialize.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Between 2006 and 2008, <a href="/wiki/2006%E2%80%932008_Lebanese_political_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2006–2008 Lebanese political protests">a series of protests</a> led by groups opposed to the pro-Western Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Fouad_Siniora" title="Fouad Siniora">Fouad Siniora</a> demanded the creation of a national unity government, over which the mostly Shia opposition groups would have veto power. When <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Lahoud" title="Émile Lahoud">Émile Lahoud</a>'s presidential term ended in October 2007, the opposition refused to vote for a successor unless a power-sharing deal was reached, leaving Lebanon without a president. </p>
      <p>On 9 May 2008, <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amal_Movement" title="Amal Movement">Amal</a> forces, sparked by a government declaration that Hezbollah's communications network was illegal, seized western <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Global_Politician_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Politician-104">[98]</a></sup> leading to the <a href="/wiki/2008_conflict_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="2008 conflict in Lebanon">2008 conflict in Lebanon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup> The Lebanese government denounced the violence as a coup attempt.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> At least 62 people died in the resulting clashes between pro-government and opposition militias.<sup id="cite_ref-Doha_NYT_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doha_NYT-107">[101]</a></sup> On 21 May 2008, the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Doha_Agreement" title="Doha Agreement">Doha Agreement</a> ended the fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Global_Politician_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Politician-104">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Doha_NYT_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doha_NYT-107">[101]</a></sup> As part of the accord, which ended 18 months of political paralysis,<sup id="cite_ref-Doha_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doha-108">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michel_Suleiman" title="Michel Suleiman">Michel Suleiman</a> became president and a national unity government was established, granting a veto to the opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-Global_Politician_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Politician-104">[98]</a></sup> The agreement was a victory for opposition forces, as the government caved in to all their main demands.<sup id="cite_ref-Doha_NYT_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doha_NYT-107">[101]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Shatila_-_street_view_(3).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Shatila_-_street_view_%283%29.jpg/220px-Shatila_-_street_view_%283%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Shatila_-_street_view_%283%29.jpg/330px-Shatila_-_street_view_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Shatila_-_street_view_%283%29.jpg/440px-Shatila_-_street_view_%283%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5472" data-file-height="5472"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Shatila_-_street_view_(3).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Over 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Syrians_in_Lebanon" title="Syrians in Lebanon">Syrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_refugees" title="Palestinian refugees">Palestinian refugees</a> live in the <a href="/wiki/Shatila_refugee_camp" title="Shatila refugee camp">Shatila refugee camp</a> on the outskirts of Beirut.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In early January 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_government_of_November_2009" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese government of November 2009">national unity government</a> collapsed due to growing tensions stemming from the <a href="/wiki/Special_Tribunal_for_Lebanon" title="Special Tribunal for Lebanon">Special Tribunal for Lebanon</a>, which was expected to indict Hezbollah members for the Hariri assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_collapse_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_collapse-109">[103]</a></sup> The parliament elected <a href="/wiki/Najib_Mikati" title="Najib Mikati">Najib Mikati</a>, the candidate for the Hezbollah-led <a href="/wiki/March_8_Alliance" title="March 8 Alliance">March 8 Alliance</a>, Prime Minister of Lebanon, making him responsible for forming a new government.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_collapse_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_collapse-110">[104]</a></sup> Hezbollah leader <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Nasrallah" title="Hassan Nasrallah">Hassan Nasrallah</a> insists that Israel was responsible for the assassination of Hariri.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup> A report leaked by the <a href="/wiki/Al_Akhbar_(Lebanon)" title="Al Akhbar (Lebanon)">Al-Akhbar</a> newspaper in November 2010 stated that Hezbollah has drafted plans for a takeover of the country in the event that the Special Tribunal for Lebanon issues an indictment against its members.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" title="Syrian civil war">Syrian civil war</a> threatened to spill over in Lebanon, causing more <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war_spillover_in_Lebanon" title="Syrian civil war spillover in Lebanon">incidents of sectarian violence</a> and armed clashes between <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunnis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alawites_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Alawites in Lebanon">Alawites</a> in Tripoli.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/UNHCR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNHCR">UNHCR</a>, the number of Syrian refugees in Lebanon increased from around 250,000 in early 2013 to 1,000,000 in late 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-refugees_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-refugees-114">[108]</a></sup> In 2013, The <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Forces_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese Forces Party">Lebanese Forces Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kataeb_Party" title="Kataeb Party">Kataeb Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Free_Patriotic_Movement" title="Free Patriotic Movement">Free Patriotic Movement</a> voiced concerns that the country's sectarian based political system is being undermined by the influx of Syrian refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup> On 6 May 2015, <a href="/wiki/UNHCR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNHCR">UNHCR</a> suspended registration of Syrian refugees at the request of the Lebanese government.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup> In February 2016, the Lebanese government signed the Lebanon Compact, granting a minimum of €400 million of support for refugees and vulnerable Lebanese citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup> As of October 2016, the government estimates that the country hosts 1.5 million Syrians.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="2019.E2.80.932021_crisis"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2019–2021_crisis">2019–2021 crisis</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/2019%E2%80%932021_Lebanese_protests" title="2019–2021 Lebanese protests">2019–2021 Lebanese protests</a>
      </div> 
      <p>On 17 October 2019, the first of a series of mass civil demonstrations erupted;<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> they were initially triggered by planned taxes on gasoline, tobacco and online phone calls such as through <a href="/wiki/WhatsApp" title="WhatsApp">WhatsApp</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup> but quickly expanded into a country-wide condemnation of <a href="/wiki/Sectarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sectarian">sectarian</a> rule,<sup id="cite_ref-:2_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-125">[119]</a></sup> stagnant economy, unemployment, endemic corruption in the public sector,<sup id="cite_ref-:2_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-125">[119]</a></sup> legislation (such as banking secrecy) that is perceived to shield the ruling class from accountability<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[121]</a></sup> and failures from the government to provide basic services such as electricity, water and sanitation.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:WomenLine_RiadElSolh_19Nov2019.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WomenLine_RiadElSolh_19Nov2019.jpg/220px-WomenLine_RiadElSolh_19Nov2019.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WomenLine_RiadElSolh_19Nov2019.jpg/330px-WomenLine_RiadElSolh_19Nov2019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WomenLine_RiadElSolh_19Nov2019.jpg/440px-WomenLine_RiadElSolh_19Nov2019.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:WomenLine_RiadElSolh_19Nov2019.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Women protesters forming a line between riot police and protesters in Riad el Solh, <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>; 19 November 2019
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>As a result of the protests, Lebanon entered a political crisis, with Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Saad_Hariri" title="Saad Hariri">Saad Hariri</a> tendering his resignation and echoing protestors' demands for a government of independent specialists.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup> Other politicians targeted by the protests have remained in power. On 19 December 2019, former Minister of Education <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Diab_(minister)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hassan Diab (minister)">Hassan Diab</a> was designated the next prime minister and tasked with forming a new cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[124]</a></sup> Protests and acts of <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">civil disobedience</a> have since continued, with protesters denouncing and condemning the designation of Diab as prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[127]</a></sup> Lebanon is suffering the worst <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_liquidity_crisis" title="Lebanese liquidity crisis">economic crisis</a> in decades.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[129]</a></sup> Lebanon is the first country in the Middle East and North Africa to see its inflation rate exceed 50% for 30 consecutive days, according to Steve H. Hanke, professor of applied economics at the Johns Hopkins University.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup> On August 4 of 2020, <a href="/wiki/August_2020_Beirut_explosion" class="mw-redirect" title="August 2020 Beirut explosion">an explosion at the port of Beirut</a>, Lebanon's main port, destroyed the surrounding areas, killing more than 200 people, and injuring thousands more. The cause of the explosion was later determined to be 2,750 tonnes of ammonium nitrate that had been unsafely stored, and accidentally set on fire that Tuesday afternoon.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup> Less than a week after the explosion, on August 10, 2020, Hassan Diab, the prime minister that had been designated less than a year before, addressed the nation and announced his resignation. The manifestations continued till 2021 and Lebanese kept blocking the roads with burned tires protesting against the poverty and the economic crisis. On March 11, the caretaker minister of energy warned that Lebanon is threatened with "total darkness" at the end of March if no money was secured to buy fuel for power stations.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[132]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Lebanon" title="Geography of Lebanon">Geography of Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Qadisha_Valley,_Aerial_View_From_Qannoubine_Monastery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Qadisha_Valley%2C_Aerial_View_From_Qannoubine_Monastery.jpg/220px-Qadisha_Valley%2C_Aerial_View_From_Qannoubine_Monastery.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Qadisha_Valley%2C_Aerial_View_From_Qannoubine_Monastery.jpg/330px-Qadisha_Valley%2C_Aerial_View_From_Qannoubine_Monastery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Qadisha_Valley%2C_Aerial_View_From_Qannoubine_Monastery.jpg/440px-Qadisha_Valley%2C_Aerial_View_From_Qannoubine_Monastery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4056" data-file-height="3040"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Qadisha_Valley,_Aerial_View_From_Qannoubine_Monastery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Kadisha_Valley" title="Kadisha Valley">Kadisha Valley</a>, a view from Qannoubine Monastery
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lebanon is located in Western Asia between latitudes <a href="/wiki/33rd_parallel_north" title="33rd parallel north">33°</a> and <a href="/wiki/35th_parallel_north" title="35th parallel north">35° N</a> and longitudes <a href="/wiki/35th_meridian_east" title="35th meridian east">35°</a> and <a href="/wiki/37th_meridian_east" title="37th meridian east">37° E</a>. Its land straddles the "northwest of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_plate" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabian plate">Arabian plate</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The country's surface area is 10,452 square kilometres (4,036&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) of which 10,230 square kilometres (3,950&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi) is <a href="/wiki/Land_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Land area">land</a>. Lebanon has a coastline and border of 225 kilometres (140&nbsp;mi) on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> to the west, a 375 kilometres (233&nbsp;mi) border shared with <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> to the north and east and a 79 kilometres (49&nbsp;mi) long border with Israel to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-enbr_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enbr-140">[134]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Line_(Lebanon)" title="Blue Line (Lebanon)">border</a> with the Israeli-occupied <a href="/wiki/Golan_Heights" title="Golan Heights">Golan Heights</a> is disputed by Lebanon in a small area called <a href="/wiki/Shebaa_Farms" title="Shebaa Farms">Shebaa Farms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[135]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg/170px-Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg/255px-Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg/340px-Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="584" data-file-height="744"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Lebanon from space. Snow cover can be seen on the western <a href="/wiki/Lebanon_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanon Mountains">Mount Lebanon</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Anti-Lebanon_Mountains" title="Anti-Lebanon Mountains">Anti-Lebanon</a> mountain ranges
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lebanon is divided into four distinct <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physiographic</a> regions: the coastal plain, the <a href="/wiki/Lebanon_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanon Mountains">Lebanon mountain</a> range, the <a href="/wiki/Beqaa_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Beqaa valley">Beqaa valley</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Lebanon_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Lebanon mountains">Anti-Lebanon mountains</a>. </p>
      <p>The narrow and discontinuous <a href="/wiki/Coastal_plain" title="Coastal plain">coastal plain</a> stretches from the Syrian border in the north where it widens to form the <a href="/wiki/Akkar" class="mw-redirect" title="Akkar">Akkar</a> plain to <a href="/wiki/Naqoura" title="Naqoura">Ras al-Naqoura</a> at the border with Israel in the south. The fertile coastal plain is formed of marine sediments and river deposited <a href="/wiki/Alluvium" title="Alluvium">alluvium</a> alternating with sandy bays and rocky beaches. The Lebanon mountains rise steeply parallel to the Mediterranean coast and form a ridge of <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> that runs for most of the country's length. The mountain range varies in width between 10&nbsp;km (6&nbsp;mi) and 56&nbsp;km (35&nbsp;mi); it is carved by narrow and deep gorges. The Lebanon mountains peak at 3,088 metres (10,131&nbsp;ft) <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a> in <a href="/wiki/Qurnat_as_Sawda%27" title="Qurnat as Sawda'">Qurnat as Sawda'</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Governorate" title="North Governorate">North Lebanon</a> and gradually slope to the south before rising again to a height of 2,695 metres (8,842&nbsp;ft) in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sannine" title="Mount Sannine">Mount Sannine</a>. The Beqaa valley sits between the Lebanon mountains in the west and the Anti-Lebanon range in the east; it is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Rift_Valley" title="Great Rift Valley">Great Rift Valley</a> system. The valley is 180&nbsp;km (112&nbsp;mi) long and 10 to 26&nbsp;km (6 to 16&nbsp;mi) wide, its fertile soil is formed by alluvial deposits. The Anti-Lebanon range runs parallel to the Lebanon mountains, its highest peak is in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Hermon" title="Mount Hermon">Mount Hermon</a> at 2,814 metres (9,232&nbsp;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-enbr_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enbr-140">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The mountains of Lebanon are drained by <a href="/wiki/Intermittent_stream" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermittent stream">seasonal torrents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perennial_stream" title="Perennial stream">rivers</a> foremost of which is the 145 kilometres (90&nbsp;mi) long <a href="/wiki/Litani_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Litani river">Leontes</a> that rises in the Beqaa Valley to the west of <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Baalbek</a> and empties into the Mediterranean Sea north of Tyre.<sup id="cite_ref-enbr_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enbr-140">[134]</a></sup> Lebanon has <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_of_Lebanon" title="List of rivers of Lebanon">16 rivers</a> all of which are <a href="/wiki/Navigability" title="Navigability">non navigable</a>; 13 rivers originate from <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a> and run through the steep gorges and into the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, the other three arise in the <a href="/wiki/Beqaa_Valley" title="Beqaa Valley">Beqaa Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MOTE_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MOTE-142">[136]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate of Lebanon">Climate of Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Lebanon has a moderate <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a>. In coastal areas, winters are generally cool and rainy whilst summers are hot and humid. In more elevated areas, temperatures usually drop below freezing during the winter with heavy snow cover that remains until early summer on the higher mountaintops.<sup id="cite_ref-enbr_140-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enbr-140">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup> Although most of Lebanon receives a relatively large amount of rainfall, when measured annually in comparison to its arid surroundings, certain areas in north-eastern Lebanon receives only little because of the <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a> created by the high peaks of the western mountain range.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Lebanon" title="Wildlife of Lebanon">Wildlife of Lebanon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marine_environmental_issues_in_Lebanon" title="Marine environmental issues in Lebanon">Marine environmental issues in Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cedars02(js).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cedars02%28js%29.jpg/170px-Cedars02%28js%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cedars02%28js%29.jpg/255px-Cedars02%28js%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cedars02%28js%29.jpg/340px-Cedars02%28js%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="816" data-file-height="1250"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cedars02(js).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Cedrus_libani" title="Cedrus libani">Lebanon cedar</a> is the national emblem of Lebanon.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In ancient times, Lebanon was covered by large forests of <a href="/wiki/Cedrus_libani" title="Cedrus libani">cedar trees</a>, the national emblem of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-cedar_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cedar-145">[139]</a></sup> Millennia of deforestation have altered the hydrology in Mount Lebanon and changed the regional climate adversely.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup> As of 2012, forests covered 13.4% of the Lebanese land area;<sup id="cite_ref-WB_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WB-147">[141]</a></sup> they are under constant threat from <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a> caused by the long dry summer season.<sup id="cite_ref-IPSs_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPSs-148">[142]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon_cedar_forest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lebanon_cedar_forest.jpg/170px-Lebanon_cedar_forest.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="91" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lebanon_cedar_forest.jpg/255px-Lebanon_cedar_forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lebanon_cedar_forest.jpg/340px-Lebanon_cedar_forest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2281" data-file-height="1220"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon_cedar_forest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Cedrus_libani" title="Cedrus libani">Lebanon cedar</a> located in El-Arz, Bsharri, Lebanon..
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>As a result of longstanding exploitation, few old cedar trees remain in pockets of forests in Lebanon, but there is an active program to conserve and regenerate the forests. The Lebanese approach has emphasized natural regeneration over planting by creating the right conditions for <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a> and growth. The Lebanese state has created several nature reserves that contain cedars, including the <a href="/wiki/Al_Shouf_Cedar_Nature_Reserve" title="Al Shouf Cedar Nature Reserve">Shouf Biosphere Reserve</a>, the Jaj Cedar Reserve, the <a href="/wiki/Tannourine" title="Tannourine">Tannourine</a> Reserve, the Ammouaa and Karm Shbat Reserves in the Akkar district, and the Forest of the <a href="/wiki/Cedars_of_God" title="Cedars of God">Cedars of God</a> near <a href="/wiki/Bsharri" title="Bsharri">Bsharri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup> Lebanon had a 2019 <a href="/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index" title="Forest Landscape Integrity Index">Forest Landscape Integrity Index</a> mean score of 3.76/10, ranking it 141st globally out of 172 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FLII-Supplementary_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLII-Supplementary-152">[146]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2010, the Environment Ministry set a 10-year plan to increase the national forest coverage by 20%, which is equivalent to the planting of two million new trees each year.<sup id="cite_ref-dailystar1_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailystar1-153">[147]</a></sup> The plan, which was funded by the United States Agency for International Development (<a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">USAID</a>), and implemented by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">U.S. Forest Service</a> (USFS), through the Lebanon Reforestation Initiative (LRI), was inaugurated in 2011 by planting cedar, pine, wild almond, juniper, fir, oak and other seedlings, in ten regions around Lebanon.<sup id="cite_ref-dailystar1_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailystar1-153">[147]</a></sup> As of 2016, forests covered 13.6% of Lebanon, and other wooded lands represented a further 11%.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[148]</a></sup> Since 2011, more than 600,000 trees, including cedars and other native species, have been planted throughout Lebanon as part of the Lebanon Reforestation Initiative (LRI).<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[149]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lebanon contains two terrestrial ecoregions: <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean_conifer-sclerophyllous-broadleaf_forests" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Mediterranean conifer-sclerophyllous-broadleaf forests">Eastern Mediterranean conifer-sclerophyllous-broadleaf forests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Anatolian_montane_conifer_and_deciduous_forests" title="Southern Anatolian montane conifer and deciduous forests">Southern Anatolian montane conifer and deciduous forests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DinersteinOlson2017_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DinersteinOlson2017-156">[150]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bsharridistrictmountains.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Bsharridistrictmountains.jpg/220px-Bsharridistrictmountains.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Bsharridistrictmountains.jpg/330px-Bsharridistrictmountains.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Bsharridistrictmountains.jpg/440px-Bsharridistrictmountains.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bsharridistrictmountains.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a> is a mountain range in Lebanon. It averages above 2,500 m (8,200 ft) in elevation.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Beirut and Mount Lebanon have been facing a severe garbage crisis. After the closure of the Bourj Hammoud dump in 1997, the al-Naameh dumpsite was opened by the government in 1998. The al-Naameh dumpsite was planned to contain 2 million tons of waste for a limited period of six years at the most. It was designed to be a temporary solution, while the government would have devised a long-term plan. Sixteen years later al-Naameh was still open and exceeded its capacity by 13 million tons. In July 2015 the residents of the area, already protesting in the recent years, forced the closure of the dumpsite. The inefficiency of the government, as well as the corruption inside of the waste management company Sukleen in charge of managing the garbage in Lebanon, have resulted in piles of garbage blocking streets in Mount Lebanon and Beirut.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[151]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 2015, the Lebanese government signed an agreement with Chinook Industrial Mining, part owned by <a href="/wiki/Chinook_Sciences" title="Chinook Sciences">Chinook Sciences</a>, to export over 100,000 tons of untreated waste from <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a> and the surrounding area. The waste had accumulated in temporary locations following the government closure of the county's largest land fill site five months earlier. The contract was jointly signed with Howa International which has offices in Holland and Germany. The contract is reported to cost $212 per ton. The waste, which is compacted and infectious, would have to be sorted and was estimated to be enough to fill 2,000 containers.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[155]</a></sup> Initial reports that the waste was to be exported to <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> have been denied by diplomats.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[156]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In February 2016, the government withdrew from negotiations after it was revealed that documents relating to the export of the trash to Russia were forgeries.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[157]</a></sup> On 19 March 2016, the Cabinet reopened the Naameh landfill for 60 days in line with a plan it passed few days earlier to end the trash crisis. The plan also stipulates the establishment of landfills in <a href="/wiki/Bourj_Hammoud" title="Bourj Hammoud">Bourj Hammoud</a> and Costa Brava, east and south of Beirut respectively. Sukleen trucks began removing piled garbage from Karantina and heading to Naameh. Environment Minister Mohammad Machnouk announced during a chat with activists that more than 8,000 tons of garbage had been collected so far as part of the government's trash plan in only 24 hours. The plan's execution is still ongoing.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[159]</a></sup> In 2017, Human Rights Watch found that Lebanon's garbage crisis, and open burning of waste in particular, was posing a health risk to residents and violating the state's obligations under international law.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[160]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In September 2018, Lebanon's parliament passed a law that banned open dumping and burning of waste. Despite penalties set in case of violations, Lebanese municipalities have been openly burning the waste, putting the lives of people in danger. In October 2018, <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a> researchers witnessed the open burning of dumps in al-Qantara and <a href="/wiki/Qabrikha" title="Qabrikha">Qabrikha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[161]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On Sunday 13 October 2019 at night, a series of about 100 <a href="/wiki/Lebanon_forest_fire_2019" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanon forest fire 2019">forest fires</a> according to <a href="/wiki/Civil_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Defense">Lebanese Civil Defense</a>, broke out and spread over large areas of Lebanon's forests. Lebanese Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Saad_Hariri" title="Saad Hariri">Saad Al-Hariri</a> confirmed his contact with a number of countries to send assistance via helicopters and firefighting planes,<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup> Cyprus, Jordan, Turkey and Greece participated in firefighting. According to press reports on Tuesday (15 October), fire has decreased in different places due to the rains,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[163]</a></sup> after <a href="/wiki/Church_(building)" title="Church (building)">churches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> called on citizens to perform raining prayers.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[165]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Lebanon" title="Politics of Lebanon">Politics of Lebanon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Lebanon" title="Human rights in Lebanon">Human rights in Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Beirut_-_Downtown_-_Lebanese_parliament.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Beirut_-_Downtown_-_Lebanese_parliament.JPG/220px-Beirut_-_Downtown_-_Lebanese_parliament.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Beirut_-_Downtown_-_Lebanese_parliament.JPG/330px-Beirut_-_Downtown_-_Lebanese_parliament.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Beirut_-_Downtown_-_Lebanese_parliament.JPG/440px-Beirut_-_Downtown_-_Lebanese_parliament.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Beirut_-_Downtown_-_Lebanese_parliament.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese parliament">Lebanese parliament</a> building at the Place de l'Étoile
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Beirut_protest_in_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Beirut_protest_in_2010.jpg/220px-Beirut_protest_in_2010.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Beirut_protest_in_2010.jpg/330px-Beirut_protest_in_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Beirut_protest_in_2010.jpg/440px-Beirut_protest_in_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Beirut_protest_in_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>One of many protests in Beirut
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lebanon is a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a> that includes <a href="/wiki/Confessionalism_(politics)" title="Confessionalism (politics)">confessionalism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[166]</a></sup> in which high-ranking offices are reserved for members of specific religious groups. The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Lebanon" title="President of Lebanon">President</a>, for example, has to be a <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronite</a> Christian, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Lebanon" title="Prime Minister of Lebanon">Prime Minister</a> a <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni Muslim</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Speaker_of_Lebanon" title="Legislative Speaker of Lebanon">Speaker of the Parliament</a> a <a href="/wiki/Shi%27a_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi'a Islam in Lebanon">Shi’a Muslim</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Prime Minister">Deputy Prime Minister</a> and the Deputy Speaker of Parliament <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Eastern Orthodox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-confessional_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-confessional-173">[167]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[168]</a></sup> This system is intended to deter sectarian conflict and to represent fairly the demographic distribution of the 18 recognized religious groups in government.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[170]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Until 1975, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a> considered Lebanon to be one of only two (together with Israel) <a href="/wiki/Political_freedom" title="Political freedom">politically free</a> countries in the Middle East and North Africa region.<sup id="cite_ref-fh-historic_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fh-historic-177">[171]</a></sup> The country lost this status with the outbreak of the Civil War, and has not regained it since. Lebanon was rated "Partly Free" in 2013. Even so, Freedom House still ranks Lebanon as one of the most democratic nations in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-fh-historic_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fh-historic-177">[171]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Until 2005, <a href="/wiki/Palestinians_in_Lebanon" title="Palestinians in Lebanon">Palestinians</a> were forbidden to work in over 70 jobs because they did not have <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationality_law" title="Lebanese nationality law">Lebanese citizenship</a>. After liberalization laws were passed in 2007, the number of banned jobs dropped to around 20.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-63">[58]</a></sup> In 2010, Palestinians were granted the same rights to work as other foreigners in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[172]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lebanon's national legislature is the <a href="/wiki/Unicameral" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Lebanon" title="Parliament of Lebanon">Parliament of Lebanon</a>. Its 128 seats are <a href="/wiki/Reserved_political_positions" title="Reserved political positions">divided</a> equally between Christians and Muslims, proportionately between the 18 different denominations and proportionately between its 26 regions.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[173]</a></sup> Prior to 1990, the ratio stood at 6:5 in favor of Christians; however, the <a href="/wiki/Taif_Agreement" title="Taif Agreement">Taif Agreement</a>, which put an end to the 1975–1990 civil war, adjusted the ratio to grant equal representation to followers of the two religions.<sup id="cite_ref-confessional_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-confessional-173">[167]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Parliament is elected for a four-year term by popular vote on the basis of sectarian proportional representation.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-15">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The executive branch consists of the President, the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>, and the Prime Minister, the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>. The parliament elects the president for a non-renewable six-year term by a two-thirds majority. The president appoints the Prime Minister,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[174]</a></sup> following consultations with the parliament. The president and the prime minister form a cabinet, which must also adhere to the sectarian distribution set out by confessionalism. </p>
      <p>In an unprecedented move, the Lebanese parliament has extended its own term twice amid protests, the last being on 5 November 2014,<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[175]</a></sup> an act which comes in direct contradiction with democracy and article #42 of the Lebanese constitution as no elections have taken place.<sup id="cite_ref-constitution_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-constitution-9">[5]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lebanon was without a President between May 2014 and October 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Aoun_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aoun-182">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[177]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Nationwide elections were finally scheduled for <a href="/wiki/2018_Lebanese_general_election" title="2018 Lebanese general election">May 2018</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[178]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As of August 2019, the Lebanese cabinet included two ministers directly affiliated with <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a>, in addition to a close but officially non-member minister.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[179]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span></h3> 
      <p>There are 18 officially recognized religious groups in Lebanon, each with its own family law legislation and set of religious courts.<sup id="cite_ref-unesco1_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unesco1-186">[180]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Grand_Serail,_Government_Palace,_Beirut.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Grand_Serail%2C_Government_Palace%2C_Beirut.jpg/220px-Grand_Serail%2C_Government_Palace%2C_Beirut.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Grand_Serail%2C_Government_Palace%2C_Beirut.jpg/330px-Grand_Serail%2C_Government_Palace%2C_Beirut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Grand_Serail%2C_Government_Palace%2C_Beirut.jpg/440px-Grand_Serail%2C_Government_Palace%2C_Beirut.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4128" data-file-height="2322"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Grand_Serail,_Government_Palace,_Beirut.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Serail" title="Grand Serail">Grand Serail</a> in Beirut
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Lebanese legal system is based on the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_France" title="Law of France">French system</a>, and is a <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> country, with the exception for matters related to personal status (succession, marriage, divorce, adoption, etc.), which are governed by a separate set of laws designed for each sectarian community. For instance, the Islamic personal status laws are inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> law.<sup id="cite_ref-globalex_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globalex-187">[181]</a></sup> For Muslims, these tribunals deal with questions of marriage, divorce, custody, and inheritance and wills. For non-Muslims, personal status jurisdiction is split: the law of inheritance and wills falls under national civil jurisdiction, while Christian and Jewish religious courts are competent for marriage, divorce, and custody. Catholics can additionally appeal before the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rota" title="Roman Rota">Vatican Rota court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[182]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The most notable set of codified laws is the Code des Obligations et des Contrats promulgated in 1932 and equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/French_Civil_Code" class="mw-redirect" title="French Civil Code">French Civil Code</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-globalex_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globalex-187">[181]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">Capital punishment</a> is still de facto used to sanction certain crimes, but no longer enforced.<sup id="cite_ref-globalex_187-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globalex-187">[181]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Lebanese court system consists of three levels: courts of first instance, courts of appeal, and the court of cassation. The Constitutional Council rules on constitutionality of laws and electoral frauds. There also is a system of religious courts having jurisdiction over personal status matters within their own communities, with rules on matters such as marriage and inheritance.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-189">[183]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1990 article 95 was amended to provide that the parliament shall take necessary measures to abolish political structure based on religious affiliation, but that until such time only the highest positions in public civil service, including the judiciary, military, security forces, public and mixed institutions, shall be divided equally between Christians and Muslims without regard to the denominational affiliation within each community.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[184]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Lebanon" title="Foreign relations of Lebanon">Foreign relations of Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:UNbeirut.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/UNbeirut.jpg/220px-UNbeirut.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/UNbeirut.jpg/330px-UNbeirut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/UNbeirut.jpg/440px-UNbeirut.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:UNbeirut.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>United Nations Lebanon headquarters in Beirut
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Lebanon concluded negotiations on an association agreement with the European Union in late 2001, and both sides initialed the accord in January 2002. It is included in the European Union's <a href="/wiki/European_Neighbourhood_Policy" title="European Neighbourhood Policy">European Neighbourhood Policy</a> (ENP), which aims at bringing the EU and its neighbours closer. Lebanon also has bilateral trade agreements with several Arab states and is working toward accession to the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>. </p>
      <p>Lebanon enjoys good relations with virtually all of the other Arab countries (despite historic tensions with Libya and Syria), and hosted an <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> Summit in March 2002 for the first time in more than 35 years. Lebanon is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Francophonie" class="mw-redirect" title="Francophonie">Francophonie</a> countries and hosted the Francophonie Summit in October 2002 as well as the <a href="/wiki/Jeux_de_la_Francophonie" title="Jeux de la Francophonie">Jeux de la Francophonie</a> in <a href="/wiki/2009_Jeux_de_la_Francophonie" title="2009 Jeux de la Francophonie">2009</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Armed_Forces" title="Lebanese Armed Forces">Lebanese Armed Forces</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg/220px-Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg/330px-Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg/440px-Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="992"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Soldiers of the Lebanese army, 2009
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Armed_Forces" title="Lebanese Armed Forces">Lebanese Armed Forces (LAF)</a> has 72,000 active personnel,<sup id="cite_ref-Lebanon_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lebanon-191">[185]</a></sup> including 1,100 in the air force, and 1,000 in the navy.<sup id="cite_ref-Stinson_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stinson-192">[186]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Lebanese Armed Forces' primary missions include defending Lebanon and its citizens against external aggression, maintaining internal stability and security, confronting threats against the country's vital interests, engaging in social development activities, and undertaking relief operations in coordination with public and humanitarian institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-lafmission_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lafmission-193">[187]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Lebanon is a major recipient of foreign military aid.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[188]</a></sup> With more than $400&nbsp;million since 2005, it is the second largest per capita recipient of <a href="/wiki/United_States_military_aid" title="United States military aid">American military aid</a> behind Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[189]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="LGBT_rights">LGBT rights</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Lebanon" title="LGBT rights in Lebanon">LGBT rights in Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Male homosexuality is illegal in Lebanon.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[190]</a></sup> Discrimination against <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Lebanon" title="LGBT rights in Lebanon">LGBT people</a> in Lebanon is widespread.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[192]</a></sup> According to 2013 survey by the Pew Research Center, 80% of Lebanese respondents believe that <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a> should not be accepted by society.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[193]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Governorates_of_Lebanon" title="Governorates of Lebanon">Governorates of Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Lebanon" title="Districts of Lebanon">Districts of Lebanon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Municipalities of Lebanon">Municipalities of Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Lebanon is divided into nine <a href="/wiki/Governorate" title="Governorate">governorates</a> (<i>muḥāfaẓāt</i>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">محافظات</span>‎; singular <i><a href="/wiki/Muhafazah" title="Muhafazah">muḥāfaẓah</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">محافظة</span>‎) which are further subdivided into twenty-five districts (<i lang="ar" title="Arabic-language text">aqdyah</i>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">أقضية</span>‎; singular: <i><a href="/wiki/Qadaa" class="mw-redirect" title="Qadaa">qadāʾ</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">قضاء</span>‎).<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[194]</a></sup> The districts themselves are also divided into several municipalities, each enclosing a group of cities or villages. The governorates and their respective districts are listed below: </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Lebanon" title="Economy of Lebanon">Economy of Lebanon</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lebanon_Export_Treemap.jpg/220px-Lebanon_Export_Treemap.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lebanon_Export_Treemap.jpg/330px-Lebanon_Export_Treemap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Lebanon_Export_Treemap.jpg/440px-Lebanon_Export_Treemap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2668" data-file-height="2085"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon_Export_Treemap.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Graphical depiction of Lebanon 's product exports in 28 color-coded categories.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Lebanon" title="Constitution of Lebanon">Lebanon's constitution</a> states that 'the economic system is free and ensures private initiative and the right to private property'. Lebanon's economy follows a <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a> model.<sup id="cite_ref-expus_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-expus-201">[195]</a></sup> Most of the economy is <a href="/wiki/Dollarized" class="mw-redirect" title="Dollarized">dollarized</a>, and the country has no restrictions on the movement of capital across its borders.<sup id="cite_ref-expus_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-expus-201">[195]</a></sup> The Lebanese government's intervention in foreign trade is minimal.<sup id="cite_ref-expus_201-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-expus-201">[195]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Lebanese economy went through a significant expansion after the <a href="/wiki/Lebanon_war_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanon war 2006">war of 2006</a>, with growth averaging 9.1% between 2007 and 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-worldbank_growth_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldbank_growth-202">[196]</a></sup> After 2011 the local economy was affected by the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian civil war</a>, growing by a yearly average of 1.7% on the 2011-2016 period and by 1.5% in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-worldbank_growth_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldbank_growth-202">[196]</a></sup> In 2018, the size of the GDP was estimated to be $54.1 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_World_Fact_Book_-_Lebanon_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_World_Fact_Book_-_Lebanon-203">[197]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Seafront_Towers_at_Zaitunay_Bay,_Downtown_Beirut.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Seafront_Towers_at_Zaitunay_Bay%2C_Downtown_Beirut.jpg/220px-Seafront_Towers_at_Zaitunay_Bay%2C_Downtown_Beirut.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Seafront_Towers_at_Zaitunay_Bay%2C_Downtown_Beirut.jpg/330px-Seafront_Towers_at_Zaitunay_Bay%2C_Downtown_Beirut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Seafront_Towers_at_Zaitunay_Bay%2C_Downtown_Beirut.jpg/440px-Seafront_Towers_at_Zaitunay_Bay%2C_Downtown_Beirut.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4250" data-file-height="2552"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Seafront_Towers_at_Zaitunay_Bay,_Downtown_Beirut.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Beirut_Central_District" title="Beirut Central District">Beirut Central District</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Beirut_Souks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Beirut_Souks.jpg/220px-Beirut_Souks.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Beirut_Souks.jpg/330px-Beirut_Souks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Beirut_Souks.jpg/440px-Beirut_Souks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683"></a>
